21 /* This file defines the arrays _occidental_hershey_glyphs[] and
22  _oriental_hershey_glyphs[]. The first array includes the standard
23  (`occidental') Hershey glyphs digitized by Dr. Allen V. Hershey, some
24  glyphs due to other people, and his Japanese Hiragana and Katakana
25  glyphs. The second array includes his digitizations of Japanese
26  ideographic symbols (Kanji glyphs). It originally included the Hiragana
27  and Katakana glyphs as well, but they were moved to the first array
28  because some people may not wish to include Kanji support.
29 
30  Dr. Hershey digitized the glyphs c. 1967, at what is now the U.S. Naval
31  Surface Weapons Center in Dahlgren, Virginia. For many years he
32  distributed copies of the glyphs, and his typographic software, on
33  magnetic tape. Over 120 copies of the tape were distributed. There
34  have been many other distributions of the glyphs. In the 1970's they
35  were incorporated, not always with attribution, in several commercial
36  plotting packages. They were first freely distributed in 1985(?), by
37  being posted to Usenet (to vol. 4 of mod.sources) by Pete Holzmann
38  <pete@xc.org>, then at Octopus Enterprises. In the 1980's the glyphs
39  were incorporated in at least two freeware plotting programs, Nelson
40  Beebe's PLOT79 and Tim Pearson's PGPLOT. The latter is still available
41  (see http://astro.caltech.edu/~tjp/pgplot/ ).
42 
43  The standard hardcopy reference for the occidental Hershey glyphs, which
44  tabulates and displays them, is:
45 
46  @TechReport{Wolcott76,
47  author = {Norman M. Wolcott and Joseph Hilsenrath},
48  title = {A Contribution to Computer Typesetting Techniques:
49  Tables of Coordinates for {Hershey's} Repertory of
50  Occidental Type Fonts and Graphic Symbols},
51  institution = {U.S. National Bureau of Standards},
52  year = {1976},
53  month = {April},
54  type = {Special Publication},
55  number = {424},
56  OPTnote = {US NTIS stock number PB251845}}
57 
58  This publication is available from the NTIS (US National Technical
59  Information Service, +1 703 487 4650). The NTIS stock number is given
60  above. See also Dr. Hershey's original TR, which describes the
61  alphabets from which the glyphs were taken and gives information on the
62  oriental glyphs:
63 
64  @TechReport{Hershey67,
65  author = {Allen V. Hershey},
66  title = {Calligraphy for Computers},
67  institution = {U.S. Naval Weapons Laboratory},
68  address = {Dahlgren, VA},
69  year = {1967},
70  type = {Report},
71  number = {TR--2101},
72  month = {Aug},
73  OPTnote = {US NTIS stock number AD662398}}
74 
75  Additional references are given in the file ./doc/hershey.bib.
76 
77  The _occidental_hershey_glyphs[] array below was constructed from
78  releases of the Hershey glyphs that are later than the one described in
79  the 1976 NBS publication above. For example, Fig. 15 of that work
80  portrays 43 additional glyphs digitized by Norman Wolcott of the NBS;
81  they are included here. See the file ./doc/hershey-number for details
82  on how the array was assembled. The original Hershey glyphs, including
83  the 43 Wolcott glyphs, are stored in the 0..3999 slots of the array.
84 
85  The _occidental_hershey_glyphs[] array also includes non-Hershey glyphs
86  taken from various sources. All post-Hershey glyphs have been placed in
87  slots 4000..4194. This includes a few glyphs taken from the UGS (the
88  Unified Graphics System, developed by Bob Beach at SLAC; see Computer
89  Graphics, Fall 1974, pp. 22-23). As well, it includes some freeware
90  glyphs developed by Thomas Wolff <wolff@inf.fu-berlin.de> and
91  distributed as part of the Ghostscript distribution. The Beach and
92  Wolff glyphs are used in the Hershey Symbol fonts. Locally developed
93  glyphs (accented characters, in particular) have been added too.
94 
95  The Japanese Hiragana and Katakana glyphs digitized by Dr. Hershey,
96  formerly located among the `oriental' glyphs, are now located in slots
97  4195..4399 of the _occidental_hershey_glyphs[] array.
98 
99  The format of the glyphs in the _occidental_hershey_glyphs[] and
100  _oriental_hershey_glyphs[] arrays is the format in which the Hershey
101  glyphs were distributed to mod.sources in 1985(?) by Pete Holzmann, and
102  is due to Jim Hurt, then at Cognition Inc. Glyphs are encoded as
103  strings consisting of pairs of printable ascii characters in the range
104  0x20 to 0x7e, i.e. SP (ascii space) through ~ (ascii tilde). Each
105  character in the range [0x20,0x7e] encodes an integer. The integer may
106  be obtained from the character by subtracting 'R', i.e, 0x52. Integers
107  in the range [-0x32,0x2c], i.e., [-50,44], may be represented in this
108  way. Actually, all integers appearing in the occidental Hershey glyphs
109  are restricted to the range [-41,41], and those in the oriental glyphs
110  are restricted to the range [-12,13].
111 
112  (Note: the non-Hershey pointing hand glyphs in slots 4040..4043 of the
113  _occidental_hershey_glyphs[] array, which are due to Nelson Beebe,
114  include integers drawn from the range [-50,50]. So the four
115  corresponding strings include characters drawn from outside the
116  printable ASCII range, e.g. \204 (i.e. 0x84), which represents 50.)
117 
118  The first two characters in each string encode the x range
119  (xinit,xfinal) of a glyph. (The `width' of the glyph, as distinct from
120  the x extent of its bounding box, is xfinal - xinit.) Subsequent pairs
121  encode (x1,y1),(x2,y2),(x3,y3),..., i.e., the coordinates of points.
122  The glyph is drawn by moving to (x1,y1), stroking a vector to (x2,y2),
123  stroking a vector to (x3,y3), etc. The pair (-50,0), which is encoded
124  as the pair of characters " R", if encountered in the string is
125  interpreted as a `pen up' command: the vector immediately following
126  should be drawn with pen up, i.e., should be interpreted as a motion
127  command rather than a drawing command.
128 
129  (The pair (-50,0) could conceivably appear in the abovementioned
130  pointing hand glyphs as the coordinates of a vector endpoint, but
131  fortunately it does not. So Jim Hurt's encoding scheme works, even for
132  the four pointing hands.) */
133
